Hiloryof FROM this period the history of Scotland would

Innes, Efihy 55z. Johin

John de Fordun, who lived in the fourteenth B o O K
century, colle~ted with a pious induifry, and from I-
them picked out materials, which he formed into

It was copied in many monasteries, and the thread

through the subsequent reigns. In the beginning

Boethius published their histories of Scotland, the
former a fuccina and dry writer, the latter a co-
pious and florid one, and both equally credulous.
Not many years after, Buchanan undertook the
fame work; and if his accuracy and impartiality
had been, in any degree, equal to the elegance of
his taste, and to the purity and vigour of his style,
his history might be placed on a level with the
most admired compositions of the ancients. But,
instead of rejecting the improbable tales of Chro-
nicle writers, he was at the utmost pains to adorn
them; and hath clothed with all the beauties and
graces of fiation, those legends, which formerly
had only its wildness and extravagance.

EDWARD, having thus placed a creature of his

James Iv. JAMES IV. was naturally generous and brave;

JAMES had now not only the name, but, though

was inferior to no prince of that age in gracefulness
of person, or in vigour of mind. His understand-
ing was good, and his heart warm; the former ca-
pable of great improvement, and the latter fifcep-
tible of the best impreflions. But according to the
usual fate of Princes who are called to the throne in
their infancy, his education had been negleted.
His private preceptors were more ready to flatter,
than to infiru( him. It was the intereit of those
who governed the kingdom, to prevent him from
knowing too much. And the Earl of Angus, in
order to divert him from business, gave him an early
taste for such pleasures, as afterwards occupied and
engrof'ed him more than became a King. Accord-
ingly we discover in James all the features of a
great but uncultivated spirit. On the one hand,
violent pafifons, implacable resentment, an immo-
derate desire of power, and the utmost rage at dif-
appointment. On the other, love to his people,
zeal for the punishment of private opprefiors, con-
fidence in his favourites, and the mofit engaging
openness and affability of behaviour.

IT was in Italy, that the powerful monarchs of

France and Spain and Germany first appeared to
make a trial of their new fitrength. The division
of that country into so many finall Rates, the lux-
ury of the people, and their effeminate aversion to
arms, invited their more martial neighbours to an
easy prey. The Italiains, wvho had been accustom-
ed to mock battles only, and to decide their quar-
rels by innocent and bloodless viaories, were aflo-
nilhed at the fight of real war; and as they could
not resist the torrent, they fuffered it to take its
course, and to spend its rage. Intrigue and policy
fiupplied the want of firength; and necessity and
felf-prefervation led that ingenious people to the
great secret of modern politics, by teaching them
how to balance the power of one Prince, by
throwing that of another into the opposite scale.
By this happy device, the liberty of Italy was
long preserved. The feales were poised by very
skilful hands; the smallest variations were attend-
ed to, and no Prince was allowed to retain any fu-
periority, that could be dangerous.

A SYSTEM of conduEt, pursued with so much

success inItaly, was not long confined to that coun-
try of political refinement. The maxim of pre-
serving a balance of power is founded so much
upon obvious reasoning, and the situation of Eu-
rope rendered it so necefrary, that it soon became
a matter of chief attention to all wife politicians.
Every itep any Prince took was observed by all his

neighbours,

neighbours. Ambassadors, a kind ofhonourable B o o x
spies, authorised by the mutual jealousy of Kings, I.
resided almost conitantly at every different court,
and had it in charge to watch all its motions. Dan-
gers were foreseen at a greater difiance, and pre-
vented with more ease. Confederacies were formed
to humble any power which role above its due pro-
portion. Revenge or feif-defence were no longer
the only causes of hoflility, it became common to
take arms out of policy; and war, both in its
commencement, and in its operations, was more
an exercise of the judgment, than of the paflions
of men. Almost every war in Europe became
general, and the most inconsiderable states ac-
quired importance, because they could add weight
to either scale.

HE lays it down as a principle, agreeable to

AMONG the great and independent nobility of

Scotland, a Monarch could poffers little autho-
rity, and exercise no extensive or rigorous jurif-

fiate of property, the frequency of public com-

many quarrels and contentions. These, as we
have already observed, were decided not by law,
but by violence. The offended Baron, without

his superior authority, assembled his own follow-
ers, and invaded the lands of his rival in an hostile
manner. Together with his estate and honours,
every nobleman tranfinitted some hereditary feud
to his poflerity, who were bound in honour to
adopt and to prosecute it with the fame inveterate

rancour.

II. To suppose that the Earl of Huntly had laid any plan

for seizing the Queen, and her Ministers, seems to be no less
improbable. I. On the Queen's arrival in the horth, he ia-
boured, in good earnest, to gain her favour, and to obtain a
pardon for his son. Knox, 318. Â·2. He met the Queen, first
at Aber-dcen, and then at Rothemay, whither he would not
have ventured to come, had he harboured any such treason-
able resolution. Knox, 358. 3. His condua was irresolute

seen danger, not like one executing a concerted plan. 4. The
most considerable persons of his clan submitted to the Queen,
and found surety to obey her commands. keith, 226. Had
the Earl been previously determined to rife in aims against the
Queen, or to seize her Minifers, it is probable he would have
imparted it to his principal followers, nor would they have
deserted him in this manner.

All these circumitances leave little room to doubt of the

truth of both conspiracies. But we may observe how far this
proof, though drawn from public records, falls short, on both
sides, of legal and formal evidence. fBuchanan and Ran-
dolph, in their accounts of the conspiracy againfc Murray,
differ widely in almofl every circumitance. The accounts of

alleges, that the design of the conspirators was to carry Darnly

the Queen to St. Andrew's, and Darnly to Cafle Campbell.
The Lords, in their declaration, affirm the design of the
conspirators to have been to murder Darnly and his father,
to confine the Queen in Lochleven during life, and to ufirp
the government. To believe implicitly, whatever they find
in an ancient paper, is a folly to which Antiquaries are ex-
tremely prone. Antient papers, however, often contain no-
thing more than the slanders of a party, and the lie of the
day. The declaration of the nobles referred to, is of this
kind; it is plainly rancorous, and written in the very heat of
fadion. Many things asserted in it, are evidently false or ex-
aggerated. Let Murray and his confederates be as ambitions
as we can suppose, they mull have had some pretences, and
plausible ones too, before they conld venture to imprison their
Sovereign for life, and to seize the reins of government;
but, at that time, the Queen's condud had afforded no co-
lourable excuse for proceeding to such extremities. It is,
likewise, remarkable, that in all the proclamations against
Murray, of which so many are published in Keith, Appendix
lo8, &c. neither the violent attempt upon Darnly, nor that
which he is alleged to have formed against the Queen herself,
are ever once mentioned.

shocking to humanity, deserve our particular at-

the person who feels the injury mhould himself in-
flia the vengeance due on that account. The per-
mitting this, however, would have been deftruc-
tive to society; and punishment would have known
no bounds, either in severity, or in duration. For
this reason, in the very infancy of the social state,
the sword was taken out of private hands, and
committed to the Magiffrate. But at firit, while
laws aimed at restraining, they really fitrengthened
the principle of revenge. The earliest and molts
simple punishment for crimes was retaliation; the
offender forfeited limb for limb, and life for life.
The payment of a compensation to the person in-
jured, succeeded to the rigour of the former infli-
tution. In both these, the gratification of private
revenge was the obj eE of law ; and he who fuffer-
ed the wrong, was the only person who had a right
to pursue, to exaE, or to remit the punishment.
While laws allowed such full scope to the revenge
of one party, the intereffs of the other were not
negleEted. If the evidence of his guilt did not
amount to a full proof, or if he reckoned himself

to

B oo K to be unjufily accused, the person to whom a

IV. crime was imputed had a right to challenge his
i66. adversary to single combat, and, on obtaining the

vidtory, vindicated his own honour. In almoll
every considerable caufi, whether civil or crimi-
nal, arms were appealed to, in defence, either of
the innocence, or the property of the parties.
Juffice had seldom occasion to use her balance;
the sword alone decided every contest. The pas-
fron of revenge was nourilhed by all these means,
and grew, by daily indulgence, to be incredibly
fitrong. Mankind became habituated to Mood,
not only in times of war, but of peace ; and from
this, as well as other causes, contradedt as sax-
ing ferocity of temper and of manners. This fe-
rocity, however, made it necessary to discourage
the trial by combat; to abolish the payment of
compensations in criminal cafes; and to think of
some milder merhod of terminuting difpuces coar-
cerning civil rights. The punishments far cdtmes
became more severe, and the regulations con-

from whose protection they expe6ted impunity.
The adminifiration ofjuflice was extremely feeble

with gunpowder. The noise and ihock, which
this sudden explosion occasioned, alarmed the
whole city. The inhabitants ran to the place
whence it came. The dead body of the King,
with that of a servant who slept in the fame room,
were found lying in an adjacent garden, without
the city wall, untouched by fire, and with no
bruise or mark of violence.

l1ischarac. SUCH was the unhappy fate of Henry Stewart
ter. Lord Darnly, in the twenty-firft year of his age.

The indulgence of fortune, and his own exter-
nal accomplishments, without any other merit,
had raised him to an height of dignity, of which
he was altogether unworthy. By his folly and
ingratitude, he loft the heart of a woman who
doated on him to diftracion. His insolence
and inconstancy alienated from him such of the
nobles as had contributed molt zealously to-
wards his elevation. His levity and caprice ex-
posed him to the scorn of the people, who once
revered him as the descendant of their ancient
Kings and heroes. Had he died a natural death,
his end would have been unlamented, and his
memory have been forgotten; but the cruel
circumifances of his murder, and the remifnefs
with which it was afterwards avenged, have made
his name to be remembered with regret, and

have

have rendered him the objet of pity, to which he B o o io
had otherwise no title. 1V.

in the government of the kingdom. For this
reason, they solicited the aid of the King of
Spain, the avowed and zealous patron of Popery
in that age. Nothing could be more delightful
to the restless spirit of Philip, or more necessary
towards facilitating his schemes in the Nether-
lands, than the involving England in the con-
fusion and miseries of a civil war. The Duke of
Alva, by his diredion, encouraged the two Earls,
and promised, so soon as they either took the
field with their forces, or surprised any place of
flrength, or rescued the Queen of Scots, that he
would supply them both with money and a ifrong
body of troops. La Mothe, the Governor of
Dunkirk, in the disguise of a sailor, founded the
ports where it would be most proper to land. And
Chiapini Vitelli, one of Alva's ablell officers, was
dispatched into England, on pretence of fettling
some commercial differences between the two na-
tions; but in reality, that the rebels might be
sure of a leader of experience, as soon as they
ventured to take arms .

HAMILTON of Bothwellhaugh was the person B o o :

who conmnittcd this barbarous action. He had V.
been condemned to death soon after the battle of Lv--
Langfide, as we have already related, and owed But,5h

his estate had been bestowed upon one of the Re-
gent's favourites, who seized his house, and turn-
ed out his wife naked, in a cold night, into the
open fields, where, before next morning, ihe be-
came furiously mad. This injury made a deeper
impreflion on him than the benefit he had receiv-
ed, and from that moment he vowed to be re-
venged upon the Regent. Party- rage firengthened
and inflamed his private resentment. His kinsmen
the Hamiltons applauded the enterprise. The
maxims of that age juflified the most desperate
course he could take to obtain vengeance. He
followed the Regent for some time, and watched
for an opportunity to ifrike the blow. He resolved
at last to wait till his enemy should arrive at Lin-
lithgow, through which he was to pass in his way
from Stirling to Edinburgh. He took his itand it
a wooden gallery, which had a window towards
the ifreet; spread a feather-bed on the floor, to
hinder the noise of his feet from being heard;
hung up a black cloth behind him, that his iha-
dow might not be observed from without; and
after all this preparation, calmly expe~ted the Re-
gent's approach, who had lodged during the night
in a house not far distant. Some indiftinat in-
formation of the danger which threatened him had
been conveyed to the Regent, and he paid so much
regard to it, that he resolved to return by the fame

gare

wound*.

His charac- THERE is no person, in that age, about whom
ter. hifforians have been more divided, or whose

charaaer has been drawn with such opposite co-
lours. Personal intrepidity, military skill, fa-
gacity and vigour in the adminifiration of civil
affairs, are virtues, which even his enemies al-
low him to have poffeffed in an eminent degree.
His moral qualities are more dubious, and ought
neither to be praised, nor censured, without
great reserve, and many diftinaions. In a fierce
age, he was capable of using viatory with huma-
nity, and of treating the vanquished with mode-
ration. A patron of learning, which, among
martial nobles, was either unknown, or defpired.
Zealous for religion, to a degree, which distinguish.

ed him, even at a time when proefelons of that B o o K

rality towards them, which knew no bounds. A
disinterested passion for the liberty of his country,
prompted him to oppose the pernicious system,
which the Princes of Lorrain had obliged the
Queen-mother to pursue. On Mary's return into
Scotland, he served her with a zeal and affetion,
to which he sacrificed the friendship of those who
were most attached to his person. But, on the
other hand, his ambition was immoderate ; and
events happened that opened to him vaff projecs,
which allurcd his enterprising genius, and led him
to acions inconfiffent with the duty of a fubjea.
His treatment of the Queen, to whose bounty he
was so much indebted, was unbrotherly and un-
grateful. The dependence on Elizabeth, under
which he brought Scotland, was disgraceful to
the nation. He deceived and betraved Norfolk
with a baseness unworthy of a man of honour.
His elevation to such unexpe~led dignity inspired
him with new pallions, with haughtiness and re-
serve; and inficead of his natural manner, which
was blunt and open, he affeated the arts of dimi-
mularion and refinement. Fond, towards the end
of his life, of flattery, and impatient of advice,
his creatures, by soothing his vanity, led him
afiray, while his ancient friends stood at a distance,
and prediated his approaching fall. But, amidst
the turbulence and confusion of that factious pe-
riod, he dispensed justice with so much imparti-
ality, he repref fed the licentious borderers with so
